  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a13fd697-ce6a-445f-9c89-10bec359e7e0'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3081506321327817231">Once regarded as a brilliant eccentric whose works skirted the outer fringes of English art and literature, William Blake (1757–1827) is today recognized as a major poet, a profound thinker, and one of the most original and exciting English artists. Nowhere is his glorious poetic and pictorial legacy more evident than in <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell,</i> which many consider</span>
  <span id="freeText3081506321327817231" style="display:none">Once regarded as a brilliant eccentric whose works skirted the outer fringes of English art and literature, William Blake (1757–1827) is today recognized as a major poet, a profound thinker, and one of the most original and exciting English artists. Nowhere is his glorious poetic and pictorial legacy more evident than in <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell,</i> which many consider his most inspired and original work.<br /><i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i> is both a humorous satire on religion and morality and a work that concisely expresses Blake's essential wisdom and philosophy, much of it revealed in the 70 aphorisms of his "Proverbs of Hell." This beautiful edition, reproduced from a rare facsimile, invites readers to enjoy the rich character of Blake's own hand-printed text along with his deeply stirring illustrations, reproduced on 27 full-color plates. A typeset transcription of the text is included.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3081506321327817231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1461095435" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1461095435" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1461095435?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=philosophyland">philosophyland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=religions-myths-and-other-stories">religions-myths-and-other-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1461095435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18103630635544269475"><blockquote>
  <i>Without Contraries is no progression. Attraction and Repulsion, Reason and Energy, Love and Hate, are necessary to Human existence.<br/>From these contraries spring what the religious call Good & Evil. Good is the passive that obeys Reason. Evil is the active springing from Energy.<br/>Good is Heaven. Evil is Hell.</i>
</blockquote><br/>Besides William Blake's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/171547.Songs_of_Innocence_and_of_Experience' title='Songs of Innocence and of Experience by William Blake' rel='nofollow noopener'>Songs of Innocence and of Experience</a>, I haven't read anything else until I found this magnificent piece of writing first published in 1790. A book replete with humor,</span>
  <span id="freeText18103630635544269475" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Without Contraries is no progression. Attraction and Repulsion, Reason and Energy, Love and Hate, are necessary to Human existence.<br>From these contraries spring what the religious call Good &amp; Evil. Good is the passive that obeys Reason. Evil is the active springing from Energy.<br>Good is Heaven. Evil is Hell.</i>
</blockquote><br>Besides William Blake's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/171547.Songs_of_Innocence_and_of_Experience" title="Songs of Innocence and of Experience by William Blake" rel="nofollow noopener">Songs of Innocence and of Experience</a>, I haven't read anything else until I found this magnificent piece of writing first published in 1790. A book replete with humor, creativity and sharp observations that dissect the very essence of the Church as an institution composed of fallible men whose morality acts against human nature. The poet expresses his discontent with a system that facilitates the repression of desires - which are called "evil" but he sees them as "energy" - and imagination, among other elements that constitute the almost crushed spirits of human beings. <br><blockquote>
  <b>Prisons are built with stones of Law, Brothels with bricks of Religion.</b>
</blockquote><br>This unique work contains Blake's incisive critiques of Emanuel Swedenborg's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64280.Heaven_and_Hell" title="Heaven and Hell by Emanuel Swedenborg" rel="nofollow noopener">Heaven and Hell</a> (1758), which he considers a <i>
  <b>recapitulation of all superficial opinions, and an analysis of the more sublime, but no further.</b>
</i> Swedenborg inspired Blake's <i>Marriage</i> in which the latter also included his philosophical views on different aspects of our lives; they were magnificently portrayed in his "Proverbs of hell", one of my favorite parts.<br><blockquote>
  <b>In seed time learn, in harvest teach, in winter enjoy.</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1563121983i/27827287._SX540_.png" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><br><br>Blake's poetry and prose are accompanied by gorgeous illustrations. However, I almost went blind while trying to read that edition so I enjoyed another one with sober colors - no, not a sacrilege. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>I tell you, no virtue can exist without breaking these ten commandments; Jesus was all virtue, and acted from impulse, not from rules.</b>
</blockquote><br><br>July 14, 19<br>* Later on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18103630635544269475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer316088947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4734598204001653151">This, quite frankly, is one of the greatest pieces of literature I've ever read⁠—in fact, extend 'literature' to include philosophy and theology as well. It's mad, complicated, mystical... suffice to say, it's essentially mind-expanding.<br/><br/>Works of genius, I find, fall under two basic categories: those that form the pinnacle of a particular genre; and those that, more generally, push the very limits of human achievement. <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i> is genius in the latter sense.<br/><br/>A facsimile edit</span>
  <span id="freeText4734598204001653151" style="display:none">This, quite frankly, is one of the greatest pieces of literature I've ever read⁠—in fact, extend 'literature' to include philosophy and theology as well. It's mad, complicated, mystical... suffice to say, it's essentially mind-expanding.<br /><br />Works of genius, I find, fall under two basic categories: those that form the pinnacle of a particular genre; and those that, more generally, push the very limits of human achievement. <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i> is genius in the latter sense.<br /><br />A facsimile edition of this work is a must: as Blake's words are an extension of his images, and vice versa. (The one put out by Dover has the advantage of being inexpensive, and, crucially, in full colour.)<br /><br />Also, for further reading: check out Harold Bloom's intriguing (short) essay, "Dialectic in the Marriage of Heaven and Hell."</span>

<div id="review_439710870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/439710870" />
    <a title="Carolina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4098632-carolina"><img alt="Carolina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500894490p2/4098632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/439710870?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolina" href="/user/show/4098632-carolina">Carolina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4098632-carolina?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4098632-carolina?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4098632-carolina?shelf=england">england</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer439710870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2472288507910722546"><br/>I’ve always loved provocative poetry. Not sensationalist I-don’t-even-have-a-good-reason-to-do-this provocative poetry, but rather pondered provocation disguised in insanity. That’s what <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i> is.<br/><br/>	Blake’s conception does <b>not</b> reject religion altogether. Instead, Blake criticizes religious institution and its morality scheme, in which all is black and white. For Blake, the world has multiple shades of gray and there’s no evil or good in either side of the spectrum. In thi</span>
  <span id="freeText2472288507910722546" style="display:none"><br />I’ve always loved provocative poetry. Not sensationalist I-don’t-even-have-a-good-reason-to-do-this provocative poetry, but rather pondered provocation disguised in insanity. That’s what <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i> is.<br /><br />	Blake’s conception does <b>not</b> reject religion altogether. Instead, Blake criticizes religious institution and its morality scheme, in which all is black and white. For Blake, the world has multiple shades of gray and there’s no evil or good in either side of the spectrum. In this piece, he confers multiple good attributes to ‘hell’, such as energy, action, the power of creation through subjectivity (the human ability to be a god through every perception, “All deities reside in the human breast”), while ‘heaven’ conveys passivity, reason, restrain.<br /><br />	The ‘Proverbs of Hell’ are particularly interesting to read and think about:<br /><br />•	“A fool sees not the same tree that a wise man sees.”<br />•	“Prisons are built with stones of Law, Brothels with bricks of Religion.”<br />•	“To create a little flower is the labour of ages.”<br />•	“Improvement makes strait roads, but the crooked roads without Improvement are roads of Genius.”<br /><br />I recommend reading this as it is a very short read (even though it requires some time to reflect on each little phrase) and a very pleasant one. The liveliest piece of Blake’s I’ve read so far, and one that is easy to connect with.<br /><br />I shall end this review with a last contribution of Blake’s:<br /><i>‘Opposition is true Friendship.’</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2472288507910722546" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1729327066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3834317715918773142">I love Blake. He always manages to touch something in my heart. There's a reason the only poem I know by heart is one of his. <br/>And whether it's with words, or with paint (The Night of Enitharmon's Joy is a personal favourite), or with his etchings (another personal favourite: 'Europe supported'. At first, it looks like a picture with 3 pretty, naked girls, then you realise it is harsh social commentary, that still rings true today), he always manages to make you think.<br/><br/><br/>The Marriage of Heaven and H</span>
  <span id="freeText3834317715918773142" style="display:none">I love Blake. He always manages to touch something in my heart. There's a reason the only poem I know by heart is one of his. <br />And whether it's with words, or with paint (The Night of Enitharmon's Joy is a personal favourite), or with his etchings (another personal favourite: 'Europe supported'. At first, it looks like a picture with 3 pretty, naked girls, then you realise it is harsh social commentary, that still rings true today), he always manages to make you think.<br /><br /><br />The Marriage of Heaven and Hell is just brilliant. It's one of those books you can read a thousand times, and still discover something more, something deeper each time.<br />I thus implore you to read it. ;)<br /><br />Some quotes to live by (all from the 'Proverbs from Hell. I just selected a few that spoke to me today. There not the most well-known, just the ones I liked best today. Ask me tomorrow and I might select some others.)<br /><br />- He whose face gives no light shall never become a star.<br />- No bird soars too high if he soars with his own wings.<br />- The most sublime act is to set another before you.<br />- Always be ready to speak your mind, and a base man will avoid you.<br /><br />And the next quote summs up how Blake sees religion. My own view on it has always been similar, even before reading Blake.<br /><br />"Till a system was formed, which some took advantage of and enslaved the vulgar by attempting to realize or abstract the mental deities from their objects. Thus began Priesthood. Choosing forms of worship from poetic tales. And at length they pronounced that the Gods had ordered such things. Thus men forgot that all deities reside in the human breast."</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_83323830" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83323830" />
    <a title="Max Maxwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1959519-max-maxwell"><img alt="Max Maxwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233014368p2/1959519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/83323830?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Max Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Max Maxwell" href="/user/show/1959519-max-maxwell">Max Maxwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7491250-the-marriage-of-heaven-and-hell">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Uh... not libertarians, they'll take it out of context</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Max by: </span>
      My wife, way back when
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1959519-max-maxwell?shelf=50-book-challenge-2010">50-book-challenge-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1959519-max-maxwell?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1959519-max-maxwell?shelf=art">art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1959519-max-maxwell?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1959519-max-maxwell?shelf=religion-and-atheism">religion-and-atheism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer83323830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14336284772982014112">Hm... <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i>, eh? I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C_S__Lewis' title='C.S. Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>C.S. Lewis</a>'s <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17267.The_Great_Divorce' title='The Great Divorce by C.S. Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Great Divorce</a>
</i> before I read this, and I think his preface there sums up my thoughts on the work:<blockquote>Blake wrote The Marriage of Heaven and Hell. If I have written of their Divorce, this is not because I think myself a fit antagonist for such a genius, nor even because I feel at all sure that I know what he meant.</blockquote>And yet... I, too, <i>do</i> write of their divorce. <br/><br/>I think that <i>The Marriage</i> is a very engaging and invigorating intellectua</span>
  <span id="freeText14336284772982014112" style="display:none">Hm... <i>The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</i>, eh? I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C_S__Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">C.S. Lewis</a>'s <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17267.The_Great_Divorce" title="The Great Divorce by C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Great Divorce</a>
</i> before I read this, and I think his preface there sums up my thoughts on the work:<blockquote>Blake wrote The Marriage of Heaven and Hell. If I have written of their Divorce, this is not because I think myself a fit antagonist for such a genius, nor even because I feel at all sure that I know what he meant.</blockquote>And yet... I, too, <i>do</i> write of their divorce. <br /><br />I think that <i>The Marriage</i> is a very engaging and invigorating intellectual work, copiously and beautifully illustrated, and one of the better early interdisciplinary works in the Western canon (mingling, in 1790(!), poetry, prose, and proverbs, in a very Biblical style, though subversively so). And I love Blake's synthesis and acknowledgment of his influences—<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36217.Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg" rel="nofollow noopener">Swedenborg</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9876.John_Milton" title="John Milton" rel="nofollow noopener">Milton</a>, the Bible, and so forth. But his philosophy is, I think, dangerous, just like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/432.Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand" rel="nofollow noopener">Ayn Rand</a>'s or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3948.Aleister_Crowley" title="Aleister Crowley" rel="nofollow noopener">Aleister Crowley</a>'s, and I'll get to why in a minute. Suffice to say that whereas Rand's philosophy is <a href="http://www.superpoop.com/090909/who-is-john-galt.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">a joke</a>, debunked by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47788.Michael_Shermer" title="Michael Shermer" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Shermer</a> in one chapter of one of his books, and Crowley was clearly just trying to show Victorian society how stilted it was by taking the opposite to the extreme, Blake's is semi-plausible and very respected. The danger lies in extreme individualism, following your "energies" without restraint. In the wrong hands (those attached to simple minds), it can be taken the wrong way, and seen as permitting anything at all.<br /><br />Here's where stream of consciousness starts. Goody!<br /><br />As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13311.Al_Franken" title="Al Franken" rel="nofollow noopener">Al Franken</a> pointed out in that little book he wrote for grads—and I'm paraphrasing here—some people <i>shouldn't</i> follow their passions. What if you have a passion for embezzling large sums of money?—and so it is with Blake's "Exuberance is beauty." I understand his meaning, but is a war fought exuberantly any less brutal for it? Should all people follow their passions? <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3915.Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker" rel="nofollow noopener">Steven Pinker</a>'d be inclined to say no as well, methinks. He points out that some people are just sociopaths. Remember that guy that duped <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7927.Norman_Mailer" title="Norman Mailer" rel="nofollow noopener">Norman Mailer</a>?—but Judaism has a similar thing, Kaballah (that oft-misunderstood doctrine), wherein their are many truths revealed and those truths are seen as being dangerous, and restricted membership is enforced (well it was in the pre-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/104438.Madonna" title="Madonna" rel="nofollow noopener">Madonna</a> days).—could not the idea that both light and darkness come from Hashem be taken out of context, too?—so maybe an exuberant war can be beautiful?—no, wars are ugly affairs. I stand on my own legs here. Uh...<br /><br />Maybe the shitty thing is that, deep down, not believing in Hell isn't liberating, it's disappointing. Sure, <i>you</i> won't be burning in the afterlife, but neither will Michael Vicks... or Hitler. Shouldn't there be a Hell for the bad ones among us? There certainly is <i>evil</i>, and most of us have seen it. Isn't it crushingly disappointing that "All religions have made the error... that God will punish Man for following his energies"?(—paraphrased again.)<br /><br />And where does he get off thinking Jesus violated any of the Ten Commandments? A child could disarm Blake's Devil here, without consulting any texts. Jesus did not mock the Sabbath, any more than King David did by eating the showbread while on the run, by healing the sick thereon. The Baal Shem Tov has a nice parable illustrating that too many restrictions choke the soul, and Jesus must've concurred:<blockquote>"Listen closely," said the Besht, "I was once driving a coach with three horses—one brown, one black, and one white—and they were not able to neigh. A gentile peasant called out to me from his coach, 'Slacken the reins!' I slackened the reins, and once again the horses were able to neigh. Do you understand what I'm saying?"</blockquote>Blake's Devil's assertion that Jesus "murder'd those who were murder'd because of him" is just absurd—and "turning away the law from the woman taken in adultery" didn't condone her adultery, merely pointed out the hypocrisy of those who would punish her. He did not steal any labours to support himself; he did not bear false witness by omitting a witness before Pilate, he simply omitted making a witness at all. There's a better argument for him having coveted a normal life, but Blake doesn't mention this—his argument for Jesus having coveted is nonsense. Even if he were right on all six accounts, that's still only six out of ten!<br /><br />At the Proverbs, though, I lose my ability to comprehend. I love some and hate some. The ones I love the most are:<blockquote>The most sublime act is to set another before you.<br />If the fool would persist in his folly he would become wise.<br />Prisons are built with stones of Law, Brothels with bricks of Religion.<br />Excess of sorrow laughs. Excess of joy weeps.<br />The roaring of lions, the howling of wolves, the raging of the stormy sea, and the destructive sword, are portions of eternity too great for the eye of man.<br />The bird a nest, the spider a web, man friendship.<br />One thought fills immensity.</blockquote>But others are just perplexing. We are told that "The eagle never lost so much time. as when he submitted to learn of the crow," but then that "When thou seest an Eagle, thou seest a portion of Genius. lift up thy head!"—I can hardly disagree, but I thought the crow was the smart one here? Didn't he just teach the eagle for hours and hours?<br /><br />Others are just dangerous. "Every thing possible to be believd is an image of truth" recapitulates—sorry, predicts—the foolhardy ontological argument so readily debunked by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1194.Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins" rel="nofollow noopener">Richard Dawkins</a>; and "Where man is not nature is barren" hurts my soul so badly; it's so antithetical to everything I believe. Man is but a cog in God's nature; we have been demoted and demoted and demoted, as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10538.Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan" rel="nofollow noopener">Carl Sagan</a> shows in the "The Great Demotions" chapter of his book <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61663.Pale_Blue_Dot_A_Vision_of_the_Human_Future_in_Space" title="Pale Blue Dot A Vision of the Human Future in Space by Carl Sagan" rel="nofollow noopener">Pale Blue Dot</a>
</i>. Ask me, man's presence <i>makes</i> nature barren.<br /><br />Blake's greatest strength here is pointing out Milton's Satan is an engaging and sympathetic character, whereas his God and Messiah are abstractions, boring, revolting even. (Same as Dante's Hell vs. his purgatory and Heaven, IMO.) It's so true!<br /><br />Anyway, as you can see, I can think and have thought about this book a lot! I leave you with my favorite passage:<blockquote>The ancient Poets animated all sensible objects with Gods or Geniuses, calling them by the names and adorning them with the properties of woods, rivers, mountains, lakes, cities, nations, and whatever their enlarged &amp; numerous senses could perceive. And particularly they studied the genius of each city &amp; country, placing it under its mental deity. Till a system was formed, which some took advantage of &amp; enslav'd the vulgar by attempting to realize or abstract the mental deities from their objects; thus began Priesthood. Choosing forms of worship from poetic tales. And at length they pronounced that the gods had orderd such things. Thus men forgot that All deities reside in the human breast.</blockquote>"The Kingdom of Heaven is within you," eh?</span>
  <a data-text-id="14336284772982014112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3423601374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3423601374" />
    <a title="Felix" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22327118-felix"><img alt="Felix" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410543537p2/22327118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3423601374?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felix" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felix" href="/user/show/22327118-felix">Felix</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192811677" href="/book/show/781419.The_Marriage_of_Heaven_and_Hell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3423601374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3194400864164829738">This is a profound text, which is often frustratingly intangible. I really like William Blake - he's as trippy as anything, and is definitely a first rate poet. This particular volume contains very little in the way of poetry, and a lot in the way of theological discussion, as Blake posits the very radical idea that Christian morality need not be necessarily confined to classical dichotomies of Good vs Evil and Heaven vs Hell. Blake also rejects the strictly logical and rational, in favour of ra</span>
  <span id="freeText3194400864164829738" style="display:none">This is a profound text, which is often frustratingly intangible. I really like William Blake - he's as trippy as anything, and is definitely a first rate poet. This particular volume contains very little in the way of poetry, and a lot in the way of theological discussion, as Blake posits the very radical idea that Christian morality need not be necessarily confined to classical dichotomies of Good vs Evil and Heaven vs Hell. Blake also rejects the strictly logical and rational, in favour of radical veneration and exaltation of the poetical.<br /><br />This is certainly interesting and brain-challenging reading, but as is often the central problem of reading Blake: it is very difficult to pin down exactly what he thought. Blake, by most modern definitions, was insane. His writings do not amount to a complete philosophical or theological system. There is certainly an argument to be made that even individual works such as this one are not internally consistent. Perhaps a reliance on a highly poetical mode of expression is partly the reason for this. Blake is always beautiful but he is rarely, if ever, clear.<br /><br />Another slightly challenging about Blake, is the frequency with which he seems to position himself as a prophetic figure himself. There is little doubt that Blake saw himself in this way. Where Blake records a discussion that he had with Isaiah, this is written:<br /><br /><i>Isaiah answered: ‘I saw no God, nor heard any, in a finite organical perception; but my senses discovered the infinite in everything, and as I was then persuaded, and remain confirmed, that the voice of honest indignation is the voice of God, I cared not for consequences but wrote.’</i><br /><br />This is followed by this:<br /><br /><i>... we of Israel taught that the Poetic Genius (as you now call it) was the first principle and all the others merely derivative, which was the cause of our despising the Priests and Philosophers of other countries, ...</i><br /><br />In these it is easy to see Blake positioning both poetry and direct divine inspiration as the appopriate avenues for 'true' spirituality to be revealed. Both of those descriptions from the mouth of Isaiah can very easily be read in light of Blake's personal desire to be seen as a prophet.<br /><br />And all this talk of being a prophet is not without reasonable belief that one could become one. America in particular had a lot of Protestant prophets around this time, and would have even more in the coming nineteenth century. Blake was writing in a period in which many people were looking inwards for spiritual truth. The Catholic church had been swept away a few generations previously, and the nominally Calvinist insitution which rose in its place hardly seemed better to many Englishmen of the day. If nothing else, this new church often looked rather a lot like the old Catholic one in many of its practices. To look for spiritual truth within the soul only seemed rational, and Blake was just one of many people doing the same thing. He was also much less successful than many of the figures which have otherwise been remembered. If Blake were not such a brilliant poet, I doubt that he would be remembered as a theologian.<br /><br />The greatest challenge in Blake is that he is so unclear and inconsistent. Although definitely capable of writing beautiful poetry and prose, Blake never really achieved the clarity of expression necessary to express theological and philosophical ideas consistently. One can argue all day about whether this was because they were inconsistent in his own mind or because of a lack of discipline or skill as a technical writer (I would argue for the former), but I don't think that this is the most important question. If anyone is able to map a complete theological system in Blake's work I would be very interested to talk to them, but I myself remain skeptical that such a thing is even possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3194400864164829738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1696227505" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696227505" />
    <a title="Catalina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10427008-catalina"><img alt="Catalina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1448889434p2/10427008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696227505?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catalina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catalina" href="/user/show/10427008-catalina">Catalina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696227505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9331208591810211077"><b> "Without contraries is no progression. Attraction and repulsion, reason and energy, love and hate, are necessary to human existence".</b><br/><br/>I believe Blake wants to prove the duality of human nature and the immoral purpose of religion to "destroy existence" by trying to "reconcile the two". <br/>You'll find well known arguments by now, but revolutionary and highly controversial for the time this was published, like:<br/> - the body is not distinct from the soul;<br/> - religion is a human product used to "enslave th</span>
  <span id="freeText9331208591810211077" style="display:none"><b> "Without contraries is no progression. Attraction and repulsion, reason and energy, love and hate, are necessary to human existence".</b><br /><br />I believe Blake wants to prove the duality of human nature and the immoral purpose of religion to "destroy existence" by trying to "reconcile the two". <br />You'll find well known arguments by now, but revolutionary and highly controversial for the time this was published, like:<br /> - the body is not distinct from the soul;<br /> - religion is a human product used to "enslave the vulgar";<br /> - the need of a creator to explain infinity - "If the doors of perception were cleansed everything would appear to man as it is, infinite";<br /> - there is no virtue without sin: "Jesus was all virtue, and acted from impulse, not from rules."<br /><br />Maybe not an easy and clear read, therefore I suggest 2 reads, the second read will clear the doors of your perception, to use Blake's words :D. Impressive how well he argues his theory in just 40 something pages. Not only that, but he put forward such 'outrageous' ideas for the end of 1700s, his enlightenment is brilliant. My absolute favorite part is 'Proverbs of hell'. And if you want a taste: <br /><i> *Shame is Pride's cloak.<br /> *Prisons are built with stones of law, brothels with bricks of religion.<br /> *What is now proved was once only imagined.<br /> *You never know what is enough unless you know what is more than enough.</i><br /><br />While I think this short piece of literature is brilliant and if I'd was to judge it time wise I'd definitely give it 5*, I cannot negate the fact is nothing new to me and also lacks in form, so consequential I cannot give it more than 4*, but definitely a MUST READ!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9331208591810211077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2284523836469737585" style="display:none">After a month of reading books about mysticism and religion (both anti- and pro-) and dipping in and out of articles on the philosophy of religion, I decided to read this thing again [the rippling consequences of dating a literal Witch for a short while then angrily dismissing her entire worldview during an argument and then feeling spectacularly guilty about being so judgmental and frankly sad about no longer feeling her embrace on a couple nights a week are not to be underestimated].<br /><br /> Given the emotional investment I had when encountering the book this time around, I found it, uh, moving (?) in a way I hadn't before. <br /><br />And yeah, I'm not going to bother finding ways to put into words the level of sheer, awe-inspiring genius to be found in this text, and I'm not going to write a critical essay, so let's leave it at this. <span id="freeTextContainer1120855589832037698">"Rintrah roars and shakes his fires in the burden’d air;<br/>Hungry clouds swag on the deep."<br/><br/>Blake’s own trip to hell starts with sound and fury. Unlike Dante’s stale and frozen hell, Blake’s is a raging and flaming place of Genius and creative Energy. <br/>In this sardonic and hallucinatory poem, Blake answers to Swedenborg whose “Heaven and Hell” seems to have deeply irritated him as yet another expression of the dualism that he has always observed in institutionalised Christianity.<br/>Both Good, assimilate</span>
  <span id="freeText1120855589832037698" style="display:none">"Rintrah roars and shakes his fires in the burden’d air;<br />Hungry clouds swag on the deep."<br /><br />Blake’s own trip to hell starts with sound and fury. Unlike Dante’s stale and frozen hell, Blake’s is a raging and flaming place of Genius and creative Energy. <br />In this sardonic and hallucinatory poem, Blake answers to Swedenborg whose “Heaven and Hell” seems to have deeply irritated him as yet another expression of the dualism that he has always observed in institutionalised Christianity.<br />Both Good, assimilated to restraint, reason and soul, and Evil, coupled with energy and body, are necessary to Man and the fierce wisdom of hell is not to be disregarded.<br /><br /><br />The road of excess leads to the palace of wisdom.<br /><br />Prudence is an ugly old maid courted by Incapacity.<br /><br />He who desires but acts not, breeds pestilence.<br /><br />The roaring of lions, the howling of wolves, the raging of the stormy sea, and the destructive sword are portions of eternity too great for the eye of man.<br /><br />One thought fills immensity.</span>

<div id="review_522287615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/522287615" />
    <a title="Nancy Nguyen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6303763-nancy-nguyen"><img alt="Nancy Nguyen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316284801p2/6303763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Nguyen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Nguyen" href="/user/show/6303763-nancy-nguyen">Nancy Nguyen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer522287615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2814883811208934520">This was so great! It's filled with amazing parables and such wisdom (which can only be conjured by intertwining "good" and "evil," wink wink). I can only imagine this work being extremely revolutionary even now, so I can't imagine the reception during the Romantic period. This work makes me feel so special. Blake's like saying that we all have something to celebrate within ourselves (of course, he meant that in the most divine way possible), and you don't have to be religious to appreciate that</span>
  <span id="freeText2814883811208934520" style="display:none">This was so great! It's filled with amazing parables and such wisdom (which can only be conjured by intertwining "good" and "evil," wink wink). I can only imagine this work being extremely revolutionary even now, so I can't imagine the reception during the Romantic period. This work makes me feel so special. Blake's like saying that we all have something to celebrate within ourselves (of course, he meant that in the most divine way possible), and you don't have to be religious to appreciate that sentiment. I kept stopping and saying, "Oh, thanks, Blake. Right back at you!" Definitely recommend if you'd like to develop your personhood. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2814883811208934520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1802031409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1802031409" />
    <a title="Rima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5536925-rima"><img alt="Rima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601905257p2/5536925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1802031409?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rima" href="/user/show/5536925-rima">Rima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1802031409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8551034559800144006">Beautiful crafted plates and put together into this text. Blake's desire to rearrange the devil's relationship with God and to abolish the concept of a heaven and hell, a good and bad, is well executed but I don't know if he means for us to take him seriously. Or rather, can we take him seriously? <br/><br/>This is definitely a text that you need to experience in the format of his plates which he so painstakingly painted in his home.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_930901900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/930901900" />
    <a title="Adil El Azraki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10423661-adil-el-azraki"><img alt="Adil El Azraki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386638618p2/10423661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/930901900?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adil El Azraki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adil El Azraki" href="/user/show/10423661-adil-el-azraki">Adil El Azraki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer930901900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12159008499793958689">This, the most immediately accessible of Blake's longer works, is vigorous, deliberately outrageous, and at times comic, onslaught against many of the stock opinions of orthodox christian piety and morality. The seeming simplicity of bake's satiric attitude, however, is deceptive.<br/><br/>Initially, Blake accepts the terminology of middle-class Christian morality ("what the religious call Good and Evil") but reverses its values, In this conventional use of evil, which is manifested by the class the class</span>
  <span id="freeText12159008499793958689" style="display:none">This, the most immediately accessible of Blake's longer works, is vigorous, deliberately outrageous, and at times comic, onslaught against many of the stock opinions of orthodox christian piety and morality. The seeming simplicity of bake's satiric attitude, however, is deceptive.<br /><br />Initially, Blake accepts the terminology of middle-class Christian morality ("what the religious call Good and Evil") but reverses its values, In this conventional use of evil, which is manifested by the class the class of beings called devils and which consigns a man to the orthodox Hell, is everything associated with the body and its desires and consists essentially of energy, abundance, act, freedom. And conventional Good, which is manifested by Angels and guarantees its adherents a place in the orthodox Heaven, is associated with the Soul (regarded as entirely separate from the body) and consists of the contrary qualities of reason, restraint, passivity, and prohibition. Blandly persona (What Blake calls "the voice of the Devil) and to utter "Proverbs pf Hell." This ironic stance produces a vein of satire which is in the great 18th century tradition of sustained ironic reversal, represented by works such as Jonathan Swift's Modest Proposal.<br /><br />But the transvaluation of standard criteria is only a first stage in Blake's complex irony, designed to startle the reader into recognizing the inadequacy of standard moral categories and stock responses. As he also says in the opening summary of his total argument, "Without Contraries is no progression," and "Reason and Energy" are both "necessary to Human existence." It turns out that Blake subordinates his satiric reversal of conventional values under a more inclusive point of view, according to which the real Good, as distinguished from the merely ironic Good, is not simply freedom from restraint, but a "marriage" of the contrary extremes of desire and restraint, energy and reason, the promptings of Hell and the limitations of Heaven or as Blake calls these contraries, in the comprehensive terms he introduces in Plate 16, "the Prolific" and "whoever tries to reconcile them seeks to destroy existence." When Blake speaks not as moral satirist but as serious as the sustained conflict, without victory or suppression, of simultaneous opposites.<br /><br />Blake was stimulated to write this unique work in response to the books of the visionary Swedish theologian, Emanuel Swedenborg, whom he had at first admired but then had come to recognize as a conventional Angel in the disguise of a radical Devil. In Plate 3, the writings of Swedenborg are described as the winding clothes Blake discards as he is resurrected from the tomb of his past self, as a poet-prophet who heralds the apocalyptic promise of his age. For Blake wrote The Marriage of Heaven and Hell during the bright early years of the french revolution, when he shared the expectations of a number of radical Englishman, including the young poets Wordsworth, Coleridge, and Southey, that the Revolution was the universal violence which had been predicted by the biblical prophets as a stage immediately preceding the millennium. The double role of The Marriage as both satire and revolutionary prophecy in made explicit in A Song of Liberty, which Blake engraved in 1792 and added as a code. <br /><br /> A SONG OF LIBERTY<br /> (Plate 25, Plate 26, Plate 27)<br /><br />1. The Eternal Female groan'd! it was heard over all the Earth:<br />2. Albions coast is sick silent; the American meadows faint!<br />3. Shadows of Prophecy shiver along by the lakes and the rivers and mutter across the ocean. France rend down thy dungeon;<br />4. Golden Spain burst the barriers of old Rome;<br />5. Cast thy keys O Rome into the deep down falling, even to eternity down falling,<br />6. And weep.<br />7. In her trembling hands she took the new born terror howling;<br />8. On those infinite mountains of light, now barr'd out by the atlantic sea, the new born fire stood before the starry king!<br />9. Flag'd with grey brow'd snows and thunderous visages the jealous wings wav'd over the deep.<br />10. The speary hand burned aloft, unbuckled was the shield, forth went the hand of jealousy among the flaming hair, and (Plate 26) hurl'd the new born wonder thro' the starry night.<br />11. The fire, the fire, is falling!<br />12. Look up! look up! O citizen of London, enlarge thy countenance; O Jew, leave counting gold! return to thy oil and wine; O African! black African! (go, winged thought, widen his forehead.)<br />13. The fiery limbs, the flaming hair, shot like the sinking sun into the western sea.<br />14 Wak'd from his eternal sleep, the hoary element roaring fled away;<br />I5. Down rush'd beating his wings in vain the jealous king; his grey brow'd councellors, thunderous warriors, curl'd veterans, among helms, and shields, and chariots, horses, elephants: banners, castles, slings, and rocks,<br />I6. Falling, rushing, ruining! buried in the ruins, on Urthona's dens;<br />17. All night beneath the ruins, then their sullen flames faded emerge round the gloomy King.<br />18. With thunder and fire: leading his starry hosts thro' the waste wilderness, (Plate 27) he promulgates his ten commands, glancing: his beamy eyelids over the deep in dark dismay,<br />19. Where the son of fire in his eastern cloud, while the morning plumes her Golden breast,<br />20. Spurning the clouds written with curses, stamps the stony law to dust, loosing: the eternal horses from the dens of night, crying,<br />Empire is no more! and now the lion &amp; wolf shall cease<br /><br />Chorus<br />Let the Priests of the Raven of dawn, no longer in deadly black, with hoarse note curse the sons of joy. Nor his accepted brethren, whom tyrant, he calls free: lay the bound or build the roof. Nor pale religious letchery call that virginity, that wishes but acts not!<br />For every thing that lives is Holy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12159008499793958689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('w7LpIj2gvryjNbPnc4Pm8vN4f8PzVjT1Qnd8cnrrrMCD7m8ckD3sFmkSTOkib+Sm8J0ot137EQGmezRWYmRjKQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('w49CUPp6PIN+Go4FJzKGJF6gT0QcDB9kRzLe1j0UOwaD08RuV+duKbQ9cQt23oRwXUUYMLKhOpCjPpbyJZv07w==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It is a typical example of a Romantic closet drama. Manfred was adapted musically by Robert Schumann in 1852, in a composition entitled <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15888981238659553469\" style=\"display:none\">Manfred contains supernatural elements, in keeping with the popularity of the ghost story in England at the time. It is a typical example of a Romantic closet drama. Manfred was adapted musically by Robert Schumann in 1852, in a composition entitled Manfred: Dramatic Poem with music in Three Parts, and later by Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky in his Manfred Symphony, Op. 58, as well as by Carl Reinecke. Friedrich Nietzsche was impressed by the poem\'s depiction of a super-human being, and wrote some music for it. \n\nByron wrote this &quot;metaphysical drama&quot;, as he called it, after his marriage failed in scandal amidst charges of sexual improprieties and an incestuous affair between Byron and his half-sister, Augusta Leigh. Attacked by the press and ostracized by London society, Byron fled England for Switzerland in 1816 and never returned. Because Manfred was written immediately after this and because Manfred regards a main character tortured by his own sense of guilt for an unmentionable offense, some critics consider Manfred to be autobiographical, or even confessional.\n\nThe unnamed but forbidden nature of Manfred\'s relationship to Astarte is believed to represent Byron\'s relationship with his half-sister Augusta. Byron commenced this work in late 1816, only a few months after the famed ghost-story sessions which provided the initial impetus for Mary Shelley\'s Frankenstein. The supernatural references are made clear throughout the poem. In one scene, for example, (Act III, Scene IV, Interior of the Tower), Manfred recalls traveling through time (or astral projection traveling) to Caesar\'s palace, &quot;and fill\'d up, As \'t were Her scenic descriptions and<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1339226434579555461\" style=\"display:none\">Originally published in 1796, Mary Wollstonecraft\'s account of her trip to Denmark, Norway, and Sweden, is compelling both in its picture of countries rarely visited in Regency times and insights into Mary\'s personal life. Her scenic descriptions and political comments about Norway and her encounters with an impoverished peasantry and Danish townsfolk greedily obsessed by commerce are no less vivid than the outbursts of melancholy in these letters written to Gilbert Imlay, her unfaithful lover and father of her baby. This book attracted William Godwin to its author, who was soon to become his wife and the mother of Mary Shelley, creator of Frankenstein, making this a key work for the understanding of the Godwin-Shelley circle. This new edition is enriched by a new introduction by Sylva Norman, which puts Wollstonecraft\'s letters into their political and social context and provides enlightening information about Mary\'s They are offered here both in their origin<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12638840898239641384\" style=\"display:none\">The prose poems of the great French Symbolist, Arthur Rimbaud (1854-1891), have acquired enormous prestige among readers everywhere and have been a revolutionary influence on poetry in the twentieth century. They are offered here both in their original texts and in superb English translations by Louise Varèse. Mrs. Varèse first published her versions of Rimbaud’s Illuminations in 1946. Since then she has revised her work and has included two poems which in the interim have been reclassified as part of Illuminations. This edition also contains two other series of prose poems, which include two poems only recently discovered in France, together with an introduction in which Miss Varèse discusses the complicated ins and outs of Rimbaldien scholarship and the special qualities of Rimbaud’s writing. Rimbaud was indeed the most astonishing of French geniuses. Fired in childhood with an ambition to write, he gave up poetry before he was twenty-one. Yet he had already produced some of the finest examples of French verse. He is best known for A Season in Hell, but his other prose poems are no less remarkable. While he was working on them he spoke of his interest in hallucinations––&quot;des vertiges, des silences, des nuits.&quot; These perceptions were caught by the poet in a beam of pellucid, and       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake">About William Blake</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1199069675p3/13453.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake">William Blake
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,451
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5644772944898829753">William Blake was an English poet, painter, and printmaker. Largely unrecognised during his lifetime, Blake's work is today considered seminal and significant in the history of both poetry and the visual arts.<br/><br/>Blake's prophetic poetry has been said to form "what is in proportion to its merits the least read body of poetry in the language". His visual artistry has led one modern critic to proclaim h</span>
  <span id="freeText5644772944898829753" style="display:none">William Blake was an English poet, painter, and printmaker. Largely unrecognised during his lifetime, Blake's work is today considered seminal and significant in the history of both poetry and the visual arts.<br /><br />Blake's prophetic poetry has been said to form "what is in proportion to its merits the least read body of poetry in the language". His visual artistry has led one modern critic to proclaim him "far and away the greatest artist Britain has ever produced." Although he only once travelled any further than a day's walk outside London over the course of his life, his creative vision engendered a diverse and symbolically rich corpus, which embraced 'imagination' as "the body of God", or "Human existence itself".<br /><br />Once considered mad for his idiosyncratic views, Blake is highly regarded today for his expressiveness and creativity, and the philosophical and mystical currents that underlie his work. His work has been characterized as part of the Romantic movement, or even "Pre-Romantic", for its largely having appeared in the 18th century. Reverent of the Bible but hostile to the established Church, Blake was influenced by the ideals and ambitions of the French and American revolutions, as well as by such thinkers as Emanuel Swedenborg.<br /><br />Despite these known influences, the originality and singularity of Blake's work make it difficult to classify. One 19th century scholar characterised Blake as a "glorious luminary", "a man not forestalled by predecessors, nor to be classed with contemporaries, nor to be replaced by known or readily surmisable successors."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5644772944898829753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
